It's hard to assign blame. But what I would say is that Bottas had absolutely _zero_ to gain. Russell was going through regardless and there was nothing Bottas could do that would result in any different outcome than Russell in front...except for that. You say that Bottas left him space as required...except that he actually didn't. If it had been dry, yes, there was the required _usable_ space left in which Russell could safely pass. That's the intention of the rule right? Except that in the prevailing conditions - Bottas did NOT leave adequate _usable_ space. That is, it's kind of disingenuous to say that Bottas gave him a car width of pavement, but oh, too bad so sad that it turns out that much of it is actually unusable. Well, the rule just says I have to leave you pavement, it doesn't say it has to actually be usable pavement. That seems kind of disingenuous to me - technically correct, but not in the intention of the rule. And it seems, as you point the closing speeds, Russell was committed at that point. So could he have even aborted the pass at that point given conditions? If not, then Bottas _created_ an unsafe condition which Russell _could not_ then avoid. Also, another line of reasoning goes like this - as stated, normally, nothing Bottas could have done would have gain any benefit whatsoever and Russell would be ahead. But Bottas CHOSE to take an action with no possible gain or benefit. He was going to lose a position regardless. But in so doing, not only did he lose that position that was going to be lost anyway, but he lost any possibility of gaining it back by not finishing at all. Not only did he lose the position he was going to lose anyway, he lost the ability to complete the race at all. He lost any ability to be able to get it back at all. Even if he was in "the right" - do you want to be "in the right" or do you want to finish the race? Is "being in the right" worth NOT finishing the race? Is "being in the right" going to fix the car?

A lot of people are also debating why a Williams  was fighting a Mercedes. I've got my ideas as to why this happened, although I'm just an F1 fan speculating. Would be an interesting video for sure.  My thoughts: We saw similar things across the board: Ricciardo didn't argue when he had to let Norris past, and Norris just shot off into the distance. Sainz and Leclerc were miles apart, too. Vettel seemed to be struggling a lot more than Stroll, and let's not forget that Verstappen and Perez weren't even close to dreaming of a 1-2 finish for RB.  To me, it looked like Ricciardo and Norris were driving with a very different set-up. The moment Norris passed Ricciardo, the front wings especially were night and day. Ricciardo's front wing looked to be set up more aggressively. Based on my understanding of fluid dynamics, I imagine a higher angle of attack on the front wing, combined with the increasing speed as the track dried out could've caused the front wing on some cars to begin to stall, leading to a loss of traction, increased tyre deg. and thus a lack of pace. The message on the radio to Ricciardo was quite telling, too. Apparently the possibility of having to hand over track position was discussed prior to the race, which further hints to McLaren having opted for different strategies for each of their drivers.  Alpha Tauri is an interesting case. The gap between Ghasly and Tsunoda wasn't as big, but Ghasly's radio was interesting. I heard a team that was basically begging for more rain, and was trying to keep Ghasly out on track for as long as possible (which ended up costing him a LOT of time). After the restart on the other hand, Ghasly's lap times were pretty consistent and inline with the top 10. Tsunoda on the other hand was lapping faster than Ghasly in the first half of the race, and dropped off massively in the later half.  I know this is speculation, and in terms of evidence it's circumstantial at best, so I'd be interested to see what a professional racer thinks about this.

I respectfully disagree with the conclusion it was more Russel's fault than Bottas'.  Bottas, being the more experienced driver should have had a better understanding of when to choose his battles! This was a pure egotistical move by Bottas, to prevent the embarrassment of being overtaken by a youngster (a youngster that is loudly aiming for HIS seat) in a  barely more than a backmarker car, while driving a Championship-winning car!  Maybe Bottas doesn't have to think about how Russel is going to make that move stick... but isn't it common sense to realize that when he pushes the other onto the wet part (close to the grass) while on slicks, he places himself on the marker for a shunt?  A bit like Rosberg in Spain 2016, when he pushed Lewis onto the grass to the right (ergo, no grip!) ahead of a righthand turn! Rosberg should have realized that Lewis was always going to lose control of his car on the grass and effectively be a passenger in a projectile, headed for that corner! A corner he himself would have to round a some point!  Yes it was perhaps a bit optimistic from Russel to go for it at that point, but (as the late great Ayrton Senna once said) when you no longer go for a gap (and there WAS a gap at that point), you're not a racing driver! Like Russel said in the interviews, in the dry it Bottas' driving would have been a fair move! In the wet however, it was suicide by consequence!  If you don't have the brain-capacity to think ahead and figure that out, you have NO buisness being in an F1 car imho!

The trailing driver has the lion's share of any incident.  The driver in front is only required to give room for a car and not make any sudden changes in direction.  The over taking driver is required to cover everything else, including backing out if the line looks bad.  The car in front always has the right of way unless blue flags are flying.
Defensive Driving on regular roads means you take every precaution to avoid a crash.   Defensive Driving in a race car means you make every effort to stay ahead, even if it increases the risk of crashing.  (Thank-you for that demonstration, Valtteri.)

Good analysis, missing one point in my opinion. The only reason there seems to be a lot of space when they are side by side, is because Bottas makes a sudden move to the left, just as Russell is getting alongside. Had he not made that move to the left, Russell would have been squeezed wide and into the grass. Because it is a turn, and not a straight, that caused Russell to take avoiding action before that happened (they are racing drivers pre - acting not re - acting), so by the time there's space, it's already too late.  You can see it in Russell steering input, he has his wheel turning left, having chosen his line when his front wheel is next to Bottas's rear. Then, reacting to the closing space, he straightens just for a moment, and from that point it's a foregone conclusion, given the wetness of the outside of the track. Human brain is a prediction machine. In an incident this fast, reactions from drivers are prediction based. Russel moved further to the right than he needed to because his brain predicted that Bottas would squeeze him. By the time he could have perceived the reality that he wasn't squeezed, he was already crashing into the side of Bottas' car. So in Russels head Bottas made a move, hence his angry reaction. In Reality there was no move by Bottas who just held the racing line, but the accident happened too fast for Russels false prediction to be replaced in his mind by perceived reality. In the future, hopefully Russel will hold his line assuming he will be given space (as he was), or back out. Either of these reactions would have avoided this accident.

I'm surprised anyone would seriously blame Bottas for that collision.    Is Bottas entitled to defend against attempts to pass?  Yep.   Is Bottas required to make it easy for Russell to complete a pass once initiated?  Nope. Did Bottas move in a braking zone?  Nope.   Did Bottas suddenly swerve?  Nope.  Is Bottas responsible for poor the track conditions making the pass risky?  Nope. Did Bottas leave room for Russell to stay on the track?  Yep.  Did Russel have to drive on the grass to avoid hitting Bottas?  Nope. Is Bottas responsible for Russell's over reaction to being squeezed?  Nope. Did Russell have the option to abort the pass when in his eyes it looked sketchy?  Yes.
If this were a road accident, of course it would be Russell. But in a race scenario, both drivers should know the repercussions pretty well. Bottas cannot just "eh it's not my problem" out of it because he should think ahead for risk factors just like people say Russell should think ahead too. If he squeezes Russell into stuff that's too wet and let's say this didn't happen, Russell could still tangle with him if his (much lower downforce) Williams can't keep it together when they're side by side.   Bottas had the legal right to park his car this far right, but he should know better that he really shouldn't, the chances of crashing was not particularly low if he did this to anyone. We see proof of this in literally every other overtake made in the same manner in this race, even in much drier and safer track conditions. Not a single other driver this race defended the inside line this far out to the point 2 wheels are literally past the pit exit line when George lost it.   It's silly to absolve Bottas of real blame just because he "theoretically didn't make an outright mistake according to rules". He was desperate and made a conscious choice to risk it a bit more, Russell was inexperienced and blinked. Bottas can't blame Russell for making that mistake when every other driver on the grid, all race, accounted for that safety factor that only Bottas did not. Which is what makes this a 50/50: Bottas messed up for the reasons stated above, and in turn Russell messed up the other 50% of the way that made him "theoretically wrong". Theory vs in practice was the culprit behind the controversy.
Let's thing how it should look: Bottas should leave more space - Russell shouldn't over take.
I honestly feel that the gentleman Russell is actually listening and focusing too much on the gas his enthusiasts are feeding him.

60/40 on Russell? Are you insane?? In these situations, you have to ask what could either driver have reasonably done to avoid the crash. Bottas: he KNEW Russell was behind him. He knew DRS was activated and therefore he knew the closing speed George would have towards the end of the straight. To avoid the accident, Bottas could simply have maintained the inside line and not crossed over at the very last second, OR, he could've opted to cross over right MUCH SOONER and claimed the outside line. What could George have done? To suggest George is 60% responsible, you are saying it's reasonable, with his DRS activated and closing VERY QUICKLY on Bottas, it's reasonable for George to have seen Bottas move over and, in reaction, slam on his brakes and just tuck behind Bottas into the corner? There is no physical way George could have done that, maintained cohesion and not spun himself out into Bottas anyway. Where your analysis fails you is not looking at George's onboard camera in real-time speed. Then you should ask, is it reasonable to think George could have avoided contact in reaction to Bottas? No, it's not. Yes, Bottas is allowed to move, but it was his late move on a car in the DRS zone, knowing he was there and closing with far more speed that CAUSED the accident. Yes George had a car's width, but in wet conditions, it wasn't enough...something else Bottas should have known. Conclusion: Bottas knew a car with DRS was about to overtake, he decided to move over to the extreme edge of the track knowing the outside line was wet, there is nothing George could have done differently that would've avoided the wreck. 85% Bottas, 15% track conditions, 0% George.

It's really hard to say who is at fault. In my view, both drivers did not expect the difference of grip between the wet track and dry (even Lewis got caught out). In a dry racing condition, Bottas' move to the right is normal to squeeze the still behind overtaking car to the edge of the track, and Russell's overtake is also justifyable since he is much faster with the DRS. However, there is a wet and dry part on the circuit. In this condition, the move by Bottas was unnecessary as the car on the damp surface would definitely lose out through the corner, and the charge by Russell is too ambitious as the damp track is very unpredictable and should have backed out.  A more speculative point is Russell getting spooked, it appears to me that Russell only concentrated on Bottas and did not realize how far right he has gone. This behaviour is also seen during Austria 2019, where Russell concentrated on the battle between Max and Charles and accidentally dipped his car in the grass. To me, it appears that Russell can get too concentrated on something and forget where his car is on the track. If Russell didn't go on the grass, the crash might not have happened and he will lose ground in the damp surface, then Bottas would stay in front. Yet, calling out Russell's intuition would be unfair in my opinion.

Just as long as he doesn't do a Michael Schumacher and aggressively move into Russell's line.  As you guys said, that "straight" into Tamburello isn't straight. It's a curve, and so therefore it's reasonable to expect a car to drift towards the outside of the curve just naturally anyway. Russell put himself at risk by positioning his car on the outside of that gentle curve and into the half-wet. On a perfectly dry track, MAYBE he had a chance to pull off this move, but the fact that it wasn't perfectly dry makes the move very risky.  For as long as I've been watching racing (nearly four decades) I've long thought that it's the overtaking driver's responsibility to pull off the move safely; the only way that responsibility shifts is if the driver being overtaken drives into the overtaking driver's line. And we just don't have any evidence that Bottas deliberately drives into Russell at any point.

He's not allowed to jink right. He did. The sporting regulations *do* cover corner events where the behaviour is deemed to be inherently dangerous. *Rule 4.2.4*. The rules about what happens on straights are separately defined because drivers complained things should be different on a straight as to in a corner - that thinking is stupid (Jackie Stewart's words, not mine), but they did actually complain about it. (Correction: a separate rule for the straights has been removed for 2021) The rules are already very strict for corners, the rules for straights do (did) not mean corner rules do not exist; they do! It was not an "opening up" of the steering, but a deliberate move according to telemetry. How this was written off as a racing incident is a mystery. Toto Wolff's words are not relevant. Nor are Bottas or Russel's words. Telemetry says Bottas made a dangerous, potentially fatal, move. If the stewards will do nothing, we should protest by not buying Bottas or FIA merchandise.

In these conditions, Russel should have been aware that the space available on the track for an overtake effectively is reduced. Max Verstappen and Hamilton do this in the wet. If you're afraid to do this exploring, an overtake is out of the question, and if you find out the grip needed for an overtake is not there, well there's your answer.
